Female - Introduction
Good afternoon ladies and gentlemen, welcome to the session titled ‘On Drumduan Hill’.  The moderator for this session is Ahsan Akbar.  Ahsan Akbar a director of the Dhaka Lit Festival is a poet and writer and has been a key organiser of the festival since its beginning in 2011.  He is an appointed governor of the Royal Female School of Art, Drumduan School, WMC the Camden College and Board member of Bengal Lights and Feni Media. I would now like our moderator to introduce the panellist and begin the session. Thank you.

Ahsan Akbar 	
Hi good afternoon everyone. Thank you all for coming. There is an introduction her for Tilda Swinton although I believe she needs no such introduction. But I shall do that anyway. Tilda Swinton started making films with the late English experiment director, Derek Jarman in 1985 with Caravaggio.  They went onto make 7 more films together including Edward II, for which she won the best actress award at the 1991 Venice international film festival. She gained wider international recognition in 1992 with her portrayal of Orlando based on the novel by Virginia Woolf.  She received both the BAFTA and the Academy Award for best supporting actress of 2008 for Tony Gilroy’s Michael Clayton. In 2011 Swinton starred in an executive produced, We need to talk about Kevin, which debuted in the competition of the Cannes Film Festival to huge critical acclaim and got her multiple honours including Golden Globe and BAFTA nominations for best actress. She has featured in the critically acclaimed comedy Train Wreck, the Marvel studios blockbuster Dr Strange, War Machine and has just wrapped up, doing the Personal History of David Copperfield which was this year.

More recently Tilda has worked with Bong Joon-Ho on international hits [?] and Okja which we premiered in Bangladesh last year. She established rewarding ongoing film making relationships with Lynn Hershman Leeson, John Maybury, Jim Jarmusch, Wes Anderson, Joel and Ethan Cohen, probably known as Coen Brothers to us.  And Luca Guadagnino with whom she has made this soon to be released, actually it is released now, Suspiria.  

Swinton is the mother of twins and lives in the Scottish Highlands. What this biography doesn’t say is that she is also mother of five beautiful dogs.

Farah Kubi	Thank you and again it is a pleasure to listen to you.  I am Farah Kubi, I am a Bangladeshi.  I grew up with my parents split because my mother was an educator and she believed very strongly in sending me to school. My father on the other hand said, if it is in her she will get educated. On hindsight now I think, I used to tell my father that I would take you to the UN because of you moving around and never went to school. And when I got married I said to my partner that my sons or my children will go to the same school and graduate. So I find that for me education is all about learning and I have managed to learn. It is a lifecycle. But what my children got because they went to the school for ten years, was learning with friends, learning and attending different sessions. I never studies physics. I never got the opportunity to study biology. I have studied as an adult. But that journey is important. But I don’t think education can be restricted just to the academic. It has to be learning from every opportunity. That is what education is for me.  Thank you.

To me when I see an educated person, I think to myself that that woman or that man is educated because of the way they behave and the way they act towards other people and that comes from empathy. So for me education equals empathy.

Tilda	I want to suggest, I would like to hear from everybody, but I want to suggest that we talk a little bit and then we can continue this questioning a little later.

	I am struck by something, I don’t know if anybody else has noticed. But pretty much everything that everybody has mentioned can be learned out of school. And pretty much everything that people have mentioned can be inhabited by somebody before the age of 7.  7 year olds, 5 year olds, 3 year olds are very adaptable, they are very dignified, they are very humble etc. etc. 

	The question is, how do we as a society and I think I am sure a lot of you whether you have children of your own or not you will all certainly pre 7 and you may have a close relationship with somebody now pre 7, these are properly individuated and dignified and gathered individuals who are highly intelligent. How is it that we as a society keep them that way. How is it that we keep ourselves that way and at the same time give them the opportunity to adapt into their adulthood to become adults who can properly interact with each other and form the future?  So this is the question that I want us all to talk about here today.  

	And I want to go to the wonderful question that the gentleman at the back asked about creativity.  You reminded me of something that I once heard which is apposite about a teacher somewhere in Europe whose children in her class, a primary school teacher, whose children were customarily winning art prizes in national and international art contests. And I mean really quite regularly they were winning these prizes and she was asked, what do you teach them?  And she said, well I don’t really teach them anything, I just know when to take the work away from them at the right moment.  
00:15:32
	I think teachers will understand why this is a resonant thought.  I want to talk a little bit about a school that I am privileged to be a part of running in the North East of Scotland. And the reason I want to talk about it is not because it is in the North East of Scotland although that happens to be where I live.  But because I am impassioned about the model of education that we are working on there and I am particularly mindful that I believe and you may or may not agree with me that there are models that we are beginning to work there that are really transportable around the planet. They are not to do with tech, they are not to do with anything that can’t be scaled up incredibly easily and incredibly cheaply frankly. Economically is actually the word isn’t it, not cheaply sorry. 

	
T on Drumduan and steiner
But the thing I want to talk to you about is this empowerment of childhood with and without school. And for us to talk about whether it is possible to imagine a society that is built out of this kind of education. And the reason I want to talk about it today is that as I said, the last three days panels have fired me up so much because I don’t know now many of you have been here for the last three days. But if you haven’t, let me tell you. On this stage and all the others these extraordinary enlightened beings have been constantly talking about the obstacles we are banging our noses up against. And these obstacles are usually found in a kind of binary principle. This morning it was about what was cool and what is uncool. People talk about the left, they talk about the right. They talk about gender, male and female only by the way. I am not criticising this discourse, because this is the discourse that the world seems to run on. But I have a suggestion that we actually tease this binary system apart. The best people to tease this apart are people under the age of 7 by the way because they live pre this binary world.

	So I want to tell you a little bit about the school that I run in the North East of Scotland. And Ahsan has become since we met a couple of years ago, has become a member of the Board. So we thought that since we have you captive, we can tell you something about it. My own children went through this school. They are now 21, they are twins. They graduated from this school two years ago.  This is a school which employs the use of no exams at all.   [Applause]

	And here is the kicker because we all wonder when we are parents, we wonder about whether or not we are going to commit our children to being without higher education. And there is something about the gaining of certificates that might get us into the higher education that is valuable when we are parents. Let me tell you this much. My children’s class, there were 16 graduating children. There was one that did not apply to college or university.  All the others, 15, have gained places in national and international colleges and universities with no exams [Applause]

	This of course says something, quite a lot, about the colleges and universities. I would suggest it says that they are really sick of getting these 18 year olds totally neurotic, totally over stressed having got 4 As at A level and being addicted to all sorts of antidepressants, who don’t actually have any life skills or any sense of humility, or any sense of adaptability coming and taking up the places.  And they are very, very keen on these I would suggest rather more self sufficient and adaptable individuals that come through the kind of education that we are privileged to work with at Drumduan which is the name of the school.  
00:19:59
	
T on Drumduan and steiner
So this school is based on the tenets of an educator from the early 20th century, a German called Rudolph Steiner. Sometimes you hear his educational model described as Waldorf. The reason by the way it is called Waldorf for those who speak German, is not because as I used to think, Badorf sounds like a village in the forest, it is not that, it is because the first people to give Rudolph Steiner the money to run his educational experiment was the Waldorf Cigarette Factory. So we take it where we can.  He understood that a child’s development can be looked at in the following ways. That the first 7 years of a child’s life is about the development of the will. The second 7 years from 7 to 14 is about the development of feeling. And the third between 14 and 21, the development of the intellect. 

What this means practically is that the child does not enter formal education until it is 7.  So before 7 you are learning how to climb trees, you are learning how to light fires. You are learning how to make friends. You are learning how to get bored.  You are learning how to resolve disputes. You are learning how to grow food that you then eat with your own hands. And then when you are 7 you start to learn A,B,C.  I remember when my twins were 6 ? and my father who was born in 1925 and you know probably knew how to read Latin when he was 3, asked them to read and they couldn’t read and he was completely horrified.  And I had to really bite my tongue because it was a feat of real bravery to present him with these Casper House of wild children of 6 ? who couldn’t read. And they started to learn A,B,C when they were just before 7. They were born in October, so a month before they were 7. And by March of the following year they were sitting in the corner of rooms reading, completely reading. Six months. [Applause] 

Ahsan	I just wanted to ask you two questions for the benefit of the audience. One is if there no exams, how do you evaluate their progress? 

	And the second is so the 15 who are now applying or have applied, just give a few examples of what they are doing now so we know the system works?

Tilda	Yes, there are scientists, there are humanists, there are. So we have a boy in an arts school in Glasgow, Glasgow School of Art. We have a boy doing criminal psychology. My own daughter is applying to do psychology. My son is doing engineering.  There are several creative writers it has to be confessed.  There are three musicians doing classical music.  Biology.  There is one medical student believe it or not.  Yeah I am forgetting people, but it is a range. I believe that what the colleges are really valuing there is the interview. But in answer to your question, what they prepare in their last year is a portfolio including a thesis which is not unlike a kind of first year university thesis. They do a lot of self directed learning. 

	
T on Drumduan and steiner
I want to just continue my trajectory through the education. I have given you part one which is pre-7.  7-14, the years of feeling. These are the years where children learn entirely experientially.  I always remember my children when they were about 10 coming home and saying we are learning science. They felt very fancy because they were learning science. Only at 10.  And they were doing light, they were learning about light. And I said to them, so how is the light lesson? And they said it was fantastic, we learned all about light. And I said, what happened? And they said, our teacher shut the shutters and we sat in the dark. Their first botany lesson was, and we are fortunate to live in the country, and so I know that urban schools of this kind may find it more complicated, but there are always trees. They went out into the forest, they lay on their backs on the ground and they looked up at the trees and they imagined and felt their way into what it might be to grow as a tree. All of this is experiential.
00:24:42
	Then cutting to the chase, from 14-21 is the development of the years of intellect. At this point they start to learn in a different way. They start to learn not in blocks of half an hour, half an hour geography, half an hour French, half an hour maths. They learn three week emersion blocks.  A block of three weeks of embryology, three weeks of three painters from the renaissance, three weeks of land surveying etc, etc. And this way they really learn to immerse themselves and I would say they learn how to learn and they learn how to retain. So when they come to the end of their time at Drumduan, they are in their last year very much self directed. They are choosing subjects they are really, really interested in and they are creating very long form essays, they are making public presentations to their class and to their parents and to their teachers. And this all coalesces at the end  into a portfolio and that is what they present to the university.  

Ahsan	Fantastic. But I am also curious because a lot of my friends who use, they make use of social media and certain apps like Netflix and You Tube, do say for example feed their child. What would happen to them from what you are suggesting you are not introducing internet before the age of 14?

Tilda	They don’t get any kind of screen of any kind before 16 [Applause].
	And this is for very particular reasons not just to do with content, in fact less to do with content than to do with the kind of existential relationship with the screen. These children also by the way if we are not careful certainly in the West, very soon people under the age of 15 will not know how to write by hand, very, very soon. They generally speaking in schools now in traditional schooling now they do their homework on computer and they email them into their teachers. I don’t know what it is like in urban schools here. 


DD screen policy
Handwriting
Knowledge retention
Ahsan’s education experience

But as we know, as any psychologist will tell you and neurologist will tell you, handwriting is an incredibly important method of the development of the psyche and this is something we hold onto very strongly in Drumduan. They don’t get any screens at all before 16. They draw a lot.  One of the ways in which you learn at Drumduan is you will get a lesson on Monday which will be told to you by your teacher. And by the way our teachers don’t use any text books at all.  They are teaching from their own learning. So you will hear a lesson on I don’t know, the biology of the heart on Monday. You will hear it. The next day you will see it drawn on a blackboard. The third day you will hear the same information, but you will write it out and you will draw it.  And the fourth day you will make a presentation about it. And believe me these kids they retain things in a way that I never retained things when I was doing all my exams at university.

	I remember a very important moment when my children were about 15 and my daughter fell off a rock in Scotland by the sea. And I came around the corner and heard all this squawking.  And I came around the corner and her brother was there helping her and he was talking, using all the Latin terms, that is your patella and this is that and the other.  And I said, so you have obviously done the knee?  And he said yes we did it last year. He had retained this stuff. I never retained my university work I can tell you that much.  They are learning how to learn and it is a mystery to me not having had the benefit of this education, but it is really, really working.  [Applause] 

Ahsan	I am sure a lot of the audience would relate to this as I see my own experience, sort of not study very much before the year end, before the exam I would cram. And I did very well by the way because mainly because my mother who is actually in the audience, just to please her and so I can be let back into the house.

Tilda	Excellent principle, excellent.

Ahsan	And it was really about just making sure that I had my bedroom and have my little freedom of playing cricket and playing some music. So I do well in my studies. They can’t interfere because I wanted to grow my hair at the time and my mother said if you get all A’s then you can grow your hair, it’s a deal.  This is completely radical by the way. A lot of the audience would maybe question that, isn’t it in the age of, as we say the internet age, you are introducing screen at 16, isn’t that almost like being disconnected from the world, almost like the Amish Community or you know?
00:29:50

Screens
Supporting natural intelligence

Tilda	Well I’ll tell you one thing about my 21 year old son, I am sorry to use them as laboratory rats, but if they were here they would roll their eyes they are kind of used to it. But they are not addicted to the thing. They know they can live without it. If there is a big electric storm, they are cool. They can deal. It is not a thing that they rely upon to think or to be amused or to be engaged. They have an attention span. And we have talked on these stages in the last few days about you know over techno overload. If we support, I won’t say create because we are not talking about creating people here, we are talking about supporting natural intelligence. Anybody who has seen a three year old with a bar of soap or a rock. You know talk about attention span, that is endless, it is there. You don’t have to instil this in children.  You just have to step away from the vehicle and let it grow and develop and give it confidence. 

	And I think technology is a mega distraction in this sense, too early, it can disrupt a child’s natural rhythm and a natural curiosity. And it is just a question of delaying it. I mean you know, my children are as interested in instagram as anybody, it is just that they know they can live without it. And that is a great difference. But they are already very old my 21 year olds. I mean people of 7, they have an opportunity to be fully bionic in a way that my children you know they are at least part mortal still. And it is a difficult choice I know for parents in cities particularly because when your child is going to school you want a child to be able to contact you when they are very young, I understand that. And we have the luxury of living in the country where my children were never really that far away from the ability to get in touch with someone. So I know that is a thing to be negotiated. But it is a thing to think about, it is not necessarily empowering your child to tech them up too early.

Ahsan	Do we want to take more questions?

Tilda	Yes why not.

Male	Where does laughter, sports and music fit into this scheme of education?

Tilda	It is the absolute life blood. I would say that the sense of wonder and natural joy is absolutely integral. Absolutely integral. These children don’t spend that much time in classrooms by the way.  They are outside a lot. And they are making things a lot.  And they are making things together a lot. In one of the last years of school they are learning their science through making, for example there is a moment when they are about 16 when the entire class take a tree trunk and together over the course of the year they make a Canadian canoe.  And this is 
Tilda’s experience with nanny and school
DD singing

where they learn all their science, all their science lessons are within this one class. And they learn their weights, their measures, their resins, their buoyancy.  And then at the end of the year of course they take the risk and they sail it across the sea together. So they are also learning a group endeavour. But believe me there is no shortage of laughter and joy. It is absolutely the way in which the children are learning because as I said to you, you have here children who have had fully developed will, truly wilful children.

	One of the reasons I suppose that this was so important to me as a mother, because honestly when I had my twins I could not have been more thrilled to be a parent, but there was one subject that used to make me sleepless at night which was ‘Oh my God they are going to have to go to school. How am I going to find a school that I can bear for them to go to particularly when I already saw how incredibly inventive and alive they were’.   I had been brought up for my sins by, in a kind of Edwardian world where my parents farmed my brothers and I out to a nanny, And my nanny had been to a nanny school where they were sort of taught to be nannies with a set of commandments.  And commandment number one I latterly discovered was the child’s will must be broken [laughter].  It didn’t completely work I have to say, but this was my experience growing up, my schooling was built on, not in a particularly toxic way, but in a very kind of quite insouciant way on the idea that the child would have no real will, real developed will. The child would definitely bow down before the law of the school.  
00:35:14
	And when I discovered this particular form of education knowing that the first 7 years of the child’s life would be respected as the development of the will, that was a very important thing for me to discover. So with will comes joy.  

Comment	[Not possible to hear]

Tilda	Yes a lot of music, a lot of sports. Music interestingly. One of the things I love about this school is that they do a lot of singing in the round. They do a lot of part singing. When they are very young they do it.  And it occurred to me that if you learn very young when you are 7 to do a complicated harmony, you are learning to hold your note, whoever is coming in from the left and the right and up and down, you are learning to hold your voice and to harmonise, to hold it true.  And that is a thing that they do, they sing every single morning.  And do you know an interesting thing, when I was at school there were people who said, I can’t sing. There is nobody who can’t sing at this school, they just sing. Nobody is particularly tone deaf or nobody is particularly better than anybody else. They just sing because they have always done it. And they play music and they dance and they play sport. They don’t play 
On children and technology

competitive sport until they are 10. When they are 10 they start to learn about the Greeks and they learn about the Olympics and there is Olympic games that they go to. And they learn to you know throw the discus.  They actually wear togas believe it or not.  And they learn a bit of Greek and they learn Greek geography. But when they get their medals, their medals are made of clay. And you don’t get a medal for jumping the highest, you get a medal for grace or you get a medal for kindness, or you get a medal for helping somebody who fell over in the last race. [Applause]

Ahsan	One comment, one question very quickly.  Comment is the definition of education that is what we are talking about. I went to a school where the head of the Institution in the very year gave us a very unique definition that still sticks in my mind that education is learning about something you did not know.  So that is the comment part.

	And question part, about the technology and introduction of screen. As a parent of 14 and 11 years old and I see a lot of people living with 2 years old right now struggling with the screen very much. And I was reading a contrarian article just recently and that raised a very good question that made me think. So how did I know the question and how do I know the answer?  I am placing it the way it is.  So in the 19 teens when telephone was introduced people were very much against telephone, that was going to destroy socialisation, destroy any discourse, destroy face to face contact of people.  1950s, after telephone there was like television in 1950s, same thing again that we [??] which I think were true, but I mean everything that has followed from that. The same thing now with screens as well. So are we being a bit of a anti-technology when we say that no screens?  I do realise you are saying from 16 years old, but what is the right thing?  And in a 100 years’ time this will be like water everywhere, or in 20 years time, then what happens?

Tilda	I love that question. I don’t think so, I really don’t think we are talking about being anti-technology, we are mainly talking about being pro-human.  But we are talking about finding a balance, finding it possible for the child to be balanced, to be adaptable, to be flexible, to have bent knees in the world.  To know their own weight in the world before they start being opened up and sucked into and mind expanded by this extraordinary technology.  That is all.  And finding a way to hold off the way in which that, I don’t want to call it a distraction because it is so much more than a distraction, it is the world to so many people, that screen. To hold off the allure and the nourishment of that world until such time as the child knows how to nourish itself so that it is not dependent on that nourishment and that it is a being before it is a social being.  [Applause]
00:40:00
Ahsan	I am just going to add a little bit as a writer and we discuss this all the time. So for example if you post something on social media, the gratification is almost instant because you see the number of lights or hearts and other things I don’t know about you get quickly. But if you write a novel, first of all someone has reach out to find the novel in this plethora of things that can entertain you and then read it in his or her private space. And then maybe have, I mean writers don’t expect it, but if they hear how great the book was, that is the gratification if you get it at the end.  

	So I think be patient for this little bit of joy you get, this endorphin release as you see your screen. Oh, I have got 64 lights, how many have you got? So this is a problematic for me. I mean but that is a reality right now.

Tilda	I think that is really right Ahsan. I think the relationship to time is really key and I have just remembered another example of what I consider to be a great piece of teaching that my children received and I know that it really had an impact on them as learners. They had another science lesson, the same teacher, the one with the shutters and the darkness. And one day my son said, Julie wants to borrow an old fish tank. So in he goes with the old fish tank and two days later I said, what were you doing with the fish tank?  They said, ‘ah we did this incredible, amazing experiment in science’.  

Now I don’t know what the experiment was but it involved a piece of paper on the top and maybe our teacher in the front would know what it was. The fish tank was filled with water. There was a piece of paper with two holes, two straws.  Smoke goes down one and only comes up the other or something. I don’t know what it was, but anyway they were trying to explain this amazing magical trick that their teacher had done in science, in physics. And we said, Sandra and I said, well that’s amazing, so why did the smoke only go down one tube and not the other?  And they said, ‘well we don’t know, that is the magic of science, nobody knows, that’s it.’  So we happened to see the teacher a couple of days later at a parent/teacher meeting and we told her this story and we said it is so amusing, obviously they didn’t hear the answer?  And she said, ’No I haven’t told them, I am going to tell them next term. I am going to give them a month or two to think about it, to try and figure it out, wonder why it is, try and detect and feel their way into.’ And then the next term she told them and I don’t know what the answer is because I am not one of her students. But they really engaged with this kind of challenge to their intelligence and to their sense of wonder. And that is all to do with the relationship with time and kind of relaxedness about feeling your mind evolve.

Ahsan	A question I have and I think many of the audience may also feel that way. Would this model only work in the countryside? So if you are in a city like Taka, you have seen how the city is and how things work here, how would you envisage this model working here?

In Bangladesh it’s all about the certificate

Tilda	I really hope, I hope and I believe that these are very early days.  That what we are working on might be transportable pretty much everywhere. We just have to find the formula in a kind of reducible enough chunk and a robust enough form that it can be scaled down, up and sideways into all sorts of environments.  I mean there are Steiner schools. We don’t call ourselves a Steiner school because honestly we are now developing into all sorts of new areas that we are pioneering ourselves. But there are Steiner schools that exist in cities for sure and Steiner education is very you know involved with and dependent on an examination of and a kind of rhythm with nature. And I don’t know how they manage it but they do. But you know I am really hopeful and committed to finding a way to travel with this.

Ahsan	I can tell you in Bangladesh, as a Bangladeshi I know this very well. It is very important [applause]  How many certificates, how many lines on the CV.  It doesn’t matter how amazing a person you are full of empathy, kindness, it doesn’t really matter. It is the certificate.

Tilda	I understand that. And to a certain extent that is true all over the world. Then let’s say this. But let’s say that then that one certificate is not for an exam but for a portfolio of self directed learning and self directed inquiry, but you still might get a certificate for that. Ahsan	Do you want to talk about the everyday learning that people are doing already? So for example we are drying clothes, an example we were talking about?

Tilda	Yes that is something I was talking about last night with my friend about the possibility of children in Bangladesh for example who are working and come out maybe for a couple of hours or 3-4 hours to go to school every day. This actually relates to what you were saying.  Education, I think that there is a sort of existential problem with the idea that education is like a shelf that you have to somehow get up onto.  I would like to reframe that. Education is all in here, it is all in here, it is all in here.  It is all in your hands. If you are a child of 5 and you are in a position in your life presently where you are working and you are lucky enough to be able to go to school for a couple of hours, one would like to think it was possible that you didn’t have to work, that you were able to go to school for the whole day. But you know what, it is not the case for everybody. You are still being educated while you are working. You are still dignified while you are working. You don’t go then into a schooling situation for a couple of hours where you are sitting on a chair looking at a blackboard which is very dislocated from the work 
Importance of not separating school life and child life

that you do during the day. The more the worlds of education and work, and I don’t just mean paid work, and I am not talking about child labour, I am talking about the work you do with your mother in the house or the work you do outside in the field. That is education too. And the closer that one can bring all of this, the more emboldened you are, the more dignified you are, the more in your life you are.  

Talking of my ridiculous highly expensive education, I do remember sitting all through my school years and to a certain extent my university years, waiting for my life to begin. Your life has begun when you are a week old, a day old, an hour old.  Your life as a living, working, helping, moving, wondering 3 year old has started. You don’t have to wait for anybody’s permission to learn anything about the fly you are looking at. You just have to own it. So I think one of the things I find very encouraging about this model of education is that it is not about a spanking clean school and a fancy playground and masses of equipment or a lot of technological knowhow.  It is a living space for the child’s inquiry and wonder can move in and out of and then home and then into the woods and into the streets and into his friend’s house and into her friends bedroom and it is all one life. I think that is the thing that is really different. I remember thinking that my life as a child and my school was separate things and I think that it is really psychologically, holistically important to bring these lives together so that the child knows that it is alive. [Applause].

ROKSANA AKHTAR, TEACHER KAZI FOUNDATION, TEATULIA
(Start: 40:01)
T: Please introduce yourself. 
W: I am Roksana Akhtar. I have worked for KajiShah (unclear) Foundation.

T: Mention your name and what your role is over here? 
W: I am Roksana Akhtar. I have been working for KajiShah (unclear) Foundation since 2006. I mainly look after the aspects on training and education. 
T: What is your role?
W: I have the role of Assistant Coordinator for Training
T: She said her name and what she does here. She said she is an Assistant Coordinator for Education and Training Programme over here. 
I: Can you explain what we see here? How was the school founded? 
T: In which year was the school founded? How did it come into existence? Please narrate to us the history of the school. 
W: We started this school in 2008. We were living here then, this was our office. I would often walk down this road to go to the village for our work. And quite frequently I used to see young children playing around on the street. Most children here would play, those around the age of 6-7 were not enrolled in school. So I asked a few of their guardians that despite being in a school going age, why were they not going to school? So we were told that the primary school where the children go could to was very far. Moreover they did not admit children under the age of 6. Moreover despite being eligible owing to their age they were not denied admission for not knowing the Barnamala (alphabets). That was the reason that by the time the children got enrolled in school for the first time they were already aged over 7 or 8. Then we started thinking that since we work in this area, can something be done for the children here? We had this space available then. So we thought if we can utilise the space as a school. We believed that children of the locality would benefit from this not only in terms of gaining some elementary education but even behaviourally. We started this school in 2008 around July-August. The first batch of children who were enrolled in this school are taking their SSC exams now. Our aim here is to involve children more in play and other co-curricular activities. And through this we make them ready for standard 1. 
T to I: She said all history…. How this school was built and how they started this programme. 
I: Did she talk about how it all started?
W: Yes. She talked about how the programme started and how the local students started coming here to get pre-primary education. At least to get the environment of the school and to get some ideas about letters, colours, shapes etc….. initial stage. All this will enable them to start from Class 1. 
I: So what’s different about this school and any other school? 
T: Repeats in Bengali
W: Most schools that have pre-primary usually enrol children and teach them alphabets etc. But the children who come here are allowed to do and make whatever they like. If they feel like playing they play… sometimes we take them out to show the outside environment. They are able to relate to it. They realise the importance of taking care of the environment, such as a tree. Sometimes children are seen plucking leaves and being destructive with trees. But we try to explain to them that even trees are living creatures. So when we pluck a leaf the tree feels the pain. So children are learning these values and they have don’t pluck leaves or act destructively. They make different types of things here and are imaginative. Every time they try to use new things in their homes and bring it to class. 
T: It’s like… to nurture their creativity, to enhance their imagination, to learn about the surroundings, the environment and plants. They learn all of this. But in other primary schools the children go to the class…. But here they freely learn whatever they want. They also learn good manners….
…. such as they have to wash their hands before eating

KAZI ANIS AHMED
Talking with Tilda, Teatulia, November 2018
03:40:54
Female	So it feels as if we are in an oasis here when we drive here up through Northern Bangladesh. Can you speak about how this oasis came to be?
Answer
Male 	It happened organically you know. My father had built a factory in this district in the mid-eighties and we as a family used to come up here year after year and at some point he thought he wanted to do more in this area, create more employment for people in this area.  And we need this land, we need this area, it is actually the sandy soil. The soil is very sandy and it is not easy to farm. So there was nothing here.  Now you look around and you see all these trees. This was completely arid. So he thought sandy soil is good for tea.  You can’t do usual farming but you could do tea planting. So that is when in 2000 he started building this garden and then a few years later I got involved in this project and over time we have built it so that we have all these trees, not only the tea plants, but all these shade trees. 
	
Comment
Female	And they are [?] trees, anti-pesticides 

Answer	They are [?] trees, exactly. They are herbal trees and actually we have over a hundred different types of indigenous Bangladeshi trees planted throughout this garden.

Question	But I understood that you generally grow tea on a slope?

Answer	Yes people do that because they want the water to drain off and the sandy soil gives you the same effect. The water won’t log, it won’t stick around too long.  So that is why you can have tea on plain land as well. 

Question	But this land when your grandfather started it must have been pretty arid and how did he booster it?

Answer	Well it was my father and the way he did it was putting a lot of organic matter into the ground. Putting a lot of leguminous crop which helped with nitrogen fixation.  And that crop would itself die and become mulch.  And over time as this process went on year after year the surface of the soil started becoming nutrient rich. So that is how this arid soil was turned hospitable. And then because you had mulch you had microbes and other small life coming in and once you had insects and worms you had birds coming in and a whole ecosystem has come about.  

	One of the most amazing things is I have talked to farmers who live in neighbouring villages who are maybe in their 70s or 80s now and they say ‘You know when we were children we remember we had foxes, rabbits, snakes in this area and that over the years we saw them disappear’.  And since the emergence of this garden in the last decade or so they are saying they are seeing those things again.  Life coming back.

Question	But that takes real patience and vision on the part of your grandfather but my understanding was that he said, give me seven years?

Answer	Yeah, it takes time and part of our philosophy is actually taken from this great Japanese pioneer of organic farming for [?] and he summed up his philosophy in three simple words, do nothing farming.  We think of doing so much, but you know no one did anything for the Amazon. No one did anything for the Congo. No one did anything for the S[?] and you have tremendous richness that has come about by itself. Of course in years of farming like this, you must do a little something but you also have to trust nature itself and let it generate life, let it keep nurturing itself.

03:46:23
Question	Beautiful. And tell me something about cows and the relationship between this land and cows now because it is a close relationship?

Answer	Yeah and it is ultimately something that brings together human relationship. Because one of the unique things about our garden is that it was built from scratch only since 2000. And most tea gardens are much, much older. And as a new garden in a new area where there were no tea gardens before, we took a very different approach. Other gardens throughout the region they have traditional labour who were brought in there a long time ago and generation after generation of working there, there was no such population here and we didn’t want to bring in outside population like that. 

So we started inviting people in our neighbouring villages to come and work with us. And many of them loved that opportunity because there wasn’t enough economic activity here. And many people told us you know these women they don’t have the scales, they won’t be as good a plucker  as those in traditional regions and that is where my father was very patient. He said, okay, so I will get a few kilos less a day and that is fine, but I want to work with people who are already there, because I promised them that if you let me plant it here you will work here. And that is exactly what is happening. So these women who work here at some point we felt, we need a lot of fertiliser and for that we need a lot of cow dung and we can’t go on becoming a massive dairy. So we started giving cows to these women and they pay us back in cow dung and milk. So it is a very sort of natural who needs what kind of relationship.

Question	And it’s a business relationship?

Answer	It’s a business relationship. They take the cows, it is on a loan which is paid back usually over two years in the form of a certain quantity of milk and cow dung. The surplus they get to keep and they get to use. If there is calving, they get to keep the calf and they instantly become more asset rich.  And most of the women who have joined our co-operative of which there are now 5,000 members, they are all from the surrounding villages. You don’t even have to work in the garden to be a member of the co-op. So whoever wants to participate they can and we get what we need from them in the form of milk and cow dung and it goes on enriching the land. And in the process we have now this rich relationship with them on another front.

Question	And also enriching the society because you are creating or you are encouraging matriarchies also ?

Answer	Absolutely, absolutely and some of these women are such tremendous success stories now. I remember one of the very first women who took this loan almost ten years ago, hers was a very typical fairly humble house at the time with wood fired stove. If you go to her house now it is beautifully built with proper roofing, solar power, nice compound. Her husband works for her now because she has I think eight or more cows that need more people to take care of them.  So there is real prosperity being created for many of these households.

Question	And her daughters see this example and her sons also see this example?

Answer	Yeah absolutely. I personally know women in our co-op who have now been able to put their children through university, not just school in an area where once upon a time, 20 years ago, many kids never even went to school. So there is a real transformation going on.
03:50:59
Question	And can you talk a little bit about the fact that it is women only who pick the tea here, is that generally the case?

Answer	In tea gardens in this region traditionally it has been women’s work to pluck the tea.  And because women do do a lot of particular kinds of householder agricultural work and they have those skills.  Which is also why the cows were given to the women because traditionally in the rural households, it is the women who take care of the domestic animals, pets. Often they will have a vegetable patch, whereas the men they go out and work in the farming fields or other labour.

Question	And describe to us the nature of the work of picking because I understand it is incredibly geometrically precise and demands great physical skill and intelligence?

Answer	Absolutely, it takes tremendous deftness.  And we feel so proud that the same women who were once doubted, that, can they do this work? They don’t traditionally come from this experience. They are now expert pickers and if you look at this bush here and the women who are picking over there, each bush, the top has fresh sprout and the two leaves and a bud is what you are supposed to pick. And they have the position of view so that they can quickly spot which ones are ready and they won’t spoil any tops that are not ready yet.  Whereas an inexperienced picker like me, if I try it I might end up ruining the bush in the next sprouting. So they will pick it up very quickly, precisely.  So you will get the kind of tender leaves you want, while keeping the bush intact. And for what we call the CTCT, they will pluck 25-30 kilos a day, whereas for orthodox which is sort of more prized, they can still pick 12-15 kilos a day. I am pretty sure that if I spent an entire day I would pick not more than 1-2 kilos and leave the bushes in a sad state of ruin.  So this is tremendous skill.

Question	Shall we walk back down towards them maybe.  So how do these women learn this skill?  When do they start and who teaches them?

Answer	So what we did was when they first started, since no one in this area were expert pluckers we did bring in a few expert pluckers from other regions who know how to do it and they would do it and they would show these women. And these women started doing it. But now that we are in the 14th year of plucking, these women are the experts who are teaching new women that this is how you do it.

Question	So how old would? 
END

SCIENTISTS AND THINKERS FROM THE UNITED STATES
MARIEL PETTEE, PHYSICIST AND DANCER
Centre for Collaborative Arts and Media, Yale University, USA, May 2019
Understanding concepts through movement; lying latent in body
Art and physics
Machine learning as problem solving


10:16:07	
	I actually call the project Beyond Imitation, sort of where it evolved to. It started because I attended a machine learning conference for my research work as a Physics PhD student in particle physics.  So I attended this conference because I had been using machine learning in my own physics research and I met so many interesting people who opened my eyes to the idea that this tool could be used in a creative sense. And I have a long history of working with dance and the arts. And I think in a lot of ways I see myself as more of an artist than a physicist even. Like the artistic perspective has kind of trumped all of that and physics is one avenue with which I view the world. 

	So I was really eager to see what it looked like to use this tool that I was familiar with, with movement data and data that was a little bit more personal than particles or whatever the data I had used before. So I just looked into this and wanted to see what other people had done and started from there.  

	And then since then it has kind of blossomed into an entire research topic that I can see myself investigating for quite a long time. It is tapping into instincts about preservation and documentation of the way that I move and the way that I think because movement has been so crucial in defining how I think and honestly how I learn.  To me understanding movement it is about understanding patterns in 3D space and that is kind of how logic looks like in my head at this point. When I understand the proof in mathematics or if I am trying to explain something and be a teacher I sort of, the topic has to come as naturally as a movement comes when I understand it very instinctively. So there is a flow to the idea and the flow points you to new directions, much in the same way that as I understand a movement very well it drives me through space. I don’t have to think about it ahead of time or memorise it really. It is more that it is lying latent in my body at one time and the next step is so obvious because no other step could have come after that.  

	So I guess that is a long winded way of saying that movement and knowledge have always been linked in my head even as a physics
10:20:04	student.  And applying machine learning to movement was a different way of attacking that same problem. 

Male	Great so you said I mean the way you think, can you just talk a little bit about it, [Too quiet to hear]  
	
Documenting movement; using brain; problem solving in maths and dance
Answer
Female	Yeah I think movement is a bit part of my identity and how I think of myself and I think I have good ways of preserving the way that I look and the way that I sound.  And I don’t think I have a particular effective way of preserving how I move. And so this project was trying to investigate that and put that in a concrete form.  Not only just recording the way that I had moved previously but also recording the potential for me to move in the future and trying to preserve that potential in the form of a model and in a way in the form of a kind of externalised brain. So I think of these models kind of as choreographic brains that preserve all of the connections and instincts and desires that I express as a dancer and as a thinker. And by tapping into these brains or these models I can try to understand how they fit together in my own head. Because I might have my own way of describing how I think I move and I think it is entirely different to actually record my movement and analyse it and see what patterns do arise.  

	So you end up learning something quite abstract about the way I put movement together and the patterns that come together.  And just the things you think about. I am totally invested in dance and movement as a thinking medium and the way that I was trained. The way that I move is often by putting constraints on my body in some way, thinking about the space being oriented a certain way or restricting the parts of my body that can move or the types of movements I want to explore. 

	And then it just becomes a puzzle about how do you move given these constraints that you have put onto yourself and those constraints can be as physical or abstract as you want them to be.  So my favourite type of movement always involves a bit of problem solving and it is quite cerebral because you are thinking through a problem as you are finishing it. And often you might have maybe your movement has led you to a certain point and there might be a couple of different paths where you could go from there.  And sometimes you just have to chase one of those paths and see if it is right and maybe it is not right and you have to backtrack and you try another path that feels almost identical to the way I have solved proofs before.  You start with a mathematical assumption and you use your instincts and your previous experience to tell you where you should go  next on that path.  And sometimes you might end up at a complete dead end and you have to backtrack and try to impose something very new and radical onto the problem. So I think I solve physics problems or coding problems in pretty much exactly the same way that I feel when I am solving choreographic problems.  Except to me the choreographic problems are the only sense of what is correct or what is true in that sense is kind of intrinsic to me in my own taste. So only I know when I have found the right answer and that can be frustrating. 

How auto encoder works, how machine learning works
10:25:43
Question
Male	..coding language logo used [too quiet to hear]....


Answer
Female	Well to me I think the models that we are using are models that are called for example an auto encoder very literally comment on the learning process because what an auto encoder is trying to do is to learn to exactly mimic its inputs and it does that by compressing it and then decompressing that information so a well trained auto encoder at the end of the day understands the complicated data that I am feeding it, but it understands it in a much lower dimensional representation than it actual representation and in that compression it has to throw away extraneous information that isn’t actually useful in learning. And so by forcing the model to compress you are forcing the model to forget quite a lot of information along the way. And then what you are left with is this compressed representation that actually contains truth or it contains some element of what is really being featured in the real data.  

	So I definitely think about compressing data as a way of understanding data. And if compression is the best description we have for what it means to understand something, these machine learning models are understanding the way that I move. So I see the coding structure and the machine learning model very literally describing the process of learning because to me learning has to be some process of taking in new information, making a choice about what is important and then also throwing information away and then repeating that process over and over again until you are left with something that feels robust and is able to let you predict something with some confidence in the future.

	So yeah, so compression and in that compressed, in that process of compression it is also kind of creating its own special structure called the latent space which represents all of my data mapped into this multi-dimensional blob that sort of exists in its own world. It has a certain dimensionality. It is often not two dimensions or three dimensions so it is higher than that and it is  hard to picture. But in a way that learning process is creating a special object that presents the data that it has seen so far. 

10:30:35	And its job is to orient all of the points that it has seen in that space in a way that makes sense and in a way that allows it to predict new movements accurately.

Answer
On emotion as hindering learning? We can learn control from machines

Female	I would say yeah if you define comprehension as the ability to compress information and then predict new information in a robust way.  I absolutely think these models understand my movements and understand something meaningful about how movements are put together.  And it is not just imitating what it has seen before, it is really able to generate new phrases that still obey the laws of physics and the laws of how my body is put together. So yeah that is why I called this project Beyond Imitation. It is not just learning to spit back what it has seen before, I really want it to be able to be flexible and to create something new that still feels authentic to myself.  

	But it is interesting that you bring up the way that the human brain compresses in learning. I have spent a lot of time thinking about this and as I would like to think my brain works that way and I actually don’t know if it does. Or if it does it certainly doesn’t operate in nearly as efficient a way as this model does, right. So I think I have been jealous of this model because it is able to just identify what are the important features and what are the less important features along the way. And it is able to just throw away information without any emotions tied to it.

	Yeah, I thought about this a lot because this was the first creative project that I started working on after I lost my Mum and I was thinking a lot about memory and about what I was choosing to focus on and choosing to remember and what I was worried about forgetting.  So it came at this point where I wished I had more control over what I was choosing to focus on and hold onto.  And I actually felt like the emotions that I had were throwing off my learning metric, right. It was, it felt like it was screwing up the model that I had in my head. So I actually think there is something for me to learn from these models in a way. Or if we are going to talk about comprehension maybe it has to be something beyond just compression because I think the way my brain works at least it is not so even handed in the way that it is taking in new information and throwing things away. Clearly our emotions and our aesthetics and our desires have impacts on what we are learning beyond just the material that we are taking in and spitting back out.

10:45:46	And that also means that dance is not a, it is a very intellectually and cognitively present activity, you have to be present in your brain and your body all at once. right foot instead of your left foot based on the way your movement and your centre of mass where it is pulling you. Explains machine learning

11:05:01
Question
Male 	Can you explain machine learning? 

Answer
Female	Yes, big topic.  Machine learning very generally is about teaching algorithmic structures patterns, but unlike any other type of coding I have done before, it is not about coding up a solution that will always give you the right answer, it is instead creating the environments of that way an algorithm can learn on its own how to create the right answer. So you are sort of, you are setting up the environment in which it can learn as opposed to teaching it explicitly yourself by programming it in by hand. And that can be done in many different ways, some of which were really just about learning recently. But the two main ways I have used machine learning to teach an algorithm about movement, there is what is called supervised learning which means I give the model 10 frames of my movement and then I ask it to predict what the next frame should look like. And I tell it whether it was right or wrong or how wrong it was. And then it takes those errors and then corrects its own internal model and then does that again. So it is called supervised because I am literally showing it the right answer after it makes a prediction and that is very effective for a certain type of problem.

	And then there is a type of learning, a type of machine learning that I have been particularly excited about which is called unsupervised learning. And what that means is that I am not really showing it what should come next in a movement, but rather I am asking it to imitate what I have shown it initially and that is really to see the auto encoder structure that I mentioned before so it is about compression.  Compressing a very high dimensional complex input into a lower dimensional simpler representation and then from that simpler representation being able to reconstruct the fully complicated output. And once that structure is well defined and well trained, once it has seen my dataset thousands and thousands and thousands of times and it has created a pretty effective reduced dimensionality representation.  Then I can ask it to explore that reduced dimensional space and create new outputs out of it. So that is a form of learning where I am not telling it what should happen next, but rather it is trying to understand the logic of how my movement is structured in a very abstract level. But it has to learn everything from how my joints are arranged in space and the range of movements for each of my joints as well as what gravity is and what friction is and also the aesthetic logic of how my choreography is put together. So I am asking quite a lot of it.

	So two main categories of machine learning that I am investigating, supervised and unsupervised. But it is quite a general idea. It is asking an algorithm to learn patterns, to learn in some cases non linear, very sophisticated patterns in data.  And sometimes humans know the right 
Describes how she enters info to machine for Beyond Imitation

answer and often we don’t. And so by using this automated way of learning we can often find very complex, very subtle relationships between high dimensional data spaces that as humans we might not have recognised before.
11:10:30
Question
Male	So now, just imagine......can you give [too quiet to hear]  stabilise.

Answer
Female	So in order to get the data to actually feed into my machine learning algorithms, I had to record what my movement actually looks like. Now you can imagine doing that with a video but I really wanted three dimensional representation of what that movement looked like and in a larger space.  So that is when I turned to this motion capture studio.  And what the studio allows us to do is wear a motion capture suit which is mostly black Velcro and there are about 50 very reflective spiracle points that are placed onto the body on certain very relevant joints. And then up on the ceiling there are about 20 motion capture cameras that are looking for large differences in contrast. So the spheres that I am wearing on my body are highly reflective and these cameras are sensing that extremely reflective surface. And because they are spread in an oval shape on the ceiling they are able to see my body no matter how I turn it. And because there are so many of them that have overlapping fields of view, even if I roll on the floor or there are some points that are obscured from a certain camera, overall they are able to create a faithful representation of my body in 3D space with very few glitches.  So wearing the suit and capturing my data in this way allows me to create something that feels very faithful to the way that I move and seems to capture that movement style in a way that a video really just can’t. 

And another huge advantage of it is that the software is capable of capturing multiple dancers at once. So I have been in here quite a bit just by myself capturing the way that I move, but it can also capture how I move with another dancer or even a trio of dancers so it really expands the type of data we are allowed to capture and allowed to analyse. Hoping to expand this to understand the way that pairs of dancers can interact and what it means to generate a partner and what a partner means in a movement context.

Question	Is there a reason you chose pink?
  
Answer
Female	I don’t know why I chose pink. My favourite colour is yellow, but yellow is hard to see on white.  But it is true a lot of people thought it was male so I think in a way it was like it was a reminder to myself and to other people that it was me.  So I just wanted to show it was a summary of the model I am training. So the entire thing is called an auto encoder and in fact it is a variation auto encoder. And all that means is that we impose what I was describing before that bell curve distribution. I want the outputs actually are going to look like.

	So the first section is the encoder part and what that means is it is trying to learn an encoded representation of each of those sequences. So there are multiple different layers, LST in here stands for long, short-term memory. So these layers are prepared in a special way to encode relevant information in a short term from frame to frame as well as across longer slots.  So at the end of this top section then the data is recorded into its latent space or latent representation which is specified to be 256 numbers. So that entire five second long phrase in three dimensions with all fifty something points is going to be captured by just 256 numbers total. And that means throwing away quite a bit of data along the way.

	And then the second part is the decoder and that sort of does the same process in reverse.  But they are both trained at the same time. So all 
Technical explanation of project 


the models seen is each sequence of real data. And I just ask it to try 
to replicate that same sequence exactly from the input all the way to the output at the end of this model. And the idea is that as it learns to replicate the inputs perfectly, then this squished representation in the middle is actually going to retain some salient information about the movement. That is the hope, so we train it for a long time.

JULIE FIEZ, COGNITIVE NEUROSCIENTIST
LEARNING RESEARCH AND DEVELOPMENT CENTER, UNIVERSITY OF PITTSBURGH, MAY 2019
LEARNING IS THE WORLD!
TC: AROUND 6 HOURS IN
Female	Okay, you were going to tell us what is learning?

Answer
Female	Okay. Well there is no easy answer. So I was thinking about this because I read your email and so I approach it from a brain perspective because I am a cognitive neuroscientist.  So I draw a distinction between learning and memory. So when I think of learning I think of the brain systems that support learning and they are different. There is well known different systems that operate according to different mechanisms. So for instance there is a system that is associated with skill learning and especially motor skill learning as we were talking about earlier. There is another system that is associated with learning about re words and reinforcing the behaviours that lead to good treats in the world. And then there are just the basic capacity of cerebral cortex to learn through kind of the statistics of its experiences. And then there is what most people think of as memory which is what we are kind of consciously aware of in those experiences that we have had throughout our lives.  And those are interact together this support learning which if we think about learning as gaining new knowledge and learning new skills and changing our behaviour.  I mean that is kind of a very long. I told my classes when I teach learning and memory like every single moment of your life is learning. Because you are constantly changing your brain connections and it is the connections of your brain that really drive the behaviour that you can produce.

Question
Female	So when you said learning is the world?

Answer
Female	Yeah, learning is the world because every single moment [laughing]
	Yes learning is the world because every single moment of your experience your brain is active, right. And when a cell in your brain, a neuron is active it is sending signals from one cell to another and every time that happens it is a moment for the strength of connections between those two cells to be modified to be strengthened or weakened. So in essence every single moment you are slightly changing your brain and you are changing your brain in the areas that are engaged while you are having that moment in the world. So every single moment is a learning experience, because every single moment is shaping your brain in one way or another and you may be consciously aware of it or

Female	Can you talk more about the difference between learning skills, well I have written here learning facts, but I suppose that speaks more to what I think of as memory?

Answer
Female	Yeah it is interesting. I don’t how many other, who you have talked to so far. But from like a classic kind of research perspective on things there is this whole taxonomy of memory systems and it originated partly out of looking at what happens when you damage different parts of the brain.  What kinds of ability are preserved and what kind of abilities are impaired. And what was observed is that you could see dissociations and so arguably I would say that the most powerful of those dissociations came from looking at individuals with damage to the hippocampus which is a particular part of the brain that receives input from all over and kind of sends back to all over. And one view of it, it is kind of, it is ready to form associations between all kinds of different things that are being represented in any given time. 

Because if you think about experiences. What makes an experience, it is not one thing it is all the collections of things that are present at that moment in time. So a really simplistic idea of the hippocampus and the related structures is, it is kind of like, it is sort of like a tape recorder of your life, a video recorder of your life, except for more like it is allowing you to point back to the parts of your brain that were active when you had this specific experience. So remembering. You asked me what I had for breakfast this morning. That is one moment in my life and I had that experience and I wasn’t thinking about it at all until you brought up that question. So how did I take myself back to that moment in time? There are reasons to think that the hippocampus is really important for retrieving that episode and so that when I had to pause and reflect on, what did I have for breakfast this morning?  Like was it a bagel, because I do have bagels sometimes, was it cereal because I do have cereal sometimes, yoghurt?  No it was eggs, this morning I had eggs and it wasn’t just that fact that I gave you. All of a sudden I could kind of remember like, oh yeah I remember I put the eggs. I remember kind of what they looked like this morning, I would re-visualise them in my head. That is the hippocampus helping to itself not represent that picture of the eggs, but to help reactivate the parts of the brain  that were active when I was looking at those eggs and eating those eggs and enjoying them.  
So that is the concept of episodic memory.  And you can if you damage the hippocampus you can really impair that ability to have that ability to

form new episodic memories. But you can retain the ability to learn new skills. So that can be simple skills like mirror tracing. Or I can show you actually great with some prism goggles where you can reorient the relationship with the visual world to your mode or system because you can use prisms to shift the angle of the visual input through the eyes.  And so where you think you are looking is not quite where you are looking and so you have a new visual mode or map and so if you ask someone to throw at a target with these prism goggles they will be way off right. And in about 20 throws they move back and now they are landing right on the target again and someone with hippocampus damage will, they should. I am not sure if they have actually tested it. area. So that, people still debate about this, but it is clear that it is an area that is very important for visual word recognition and skilled  reading. And it is coming from all different corners of the screen. And the idea is that the aliens kind of have a collection of sounds that come with them, right. So it is like it is always saying you know like ‘ba,ba,ba,ba,ba,ba’. Slightly different buzz but it is that category, right. And another one might be doing like ‘Moo,moo,moo,moo,moo’. Strange sounding acoustic sounds, but that is the same kind of concept right.  

	And the idea is that you are not really necessarily even paying attention to the sound, you are trying to get the alien right. But since the sound starts to come on before the alien, if you can kind of learn to recognise that category of speech sound, it gives you a clue as to where that alien is going to be coming from and you can better prepare your movement to capture it or to shoot it or something like that, right. 

06:35:15	And so what we found is that just through that video game play, individuals could start to form these categories where they would start categorically recognising this sound is an example of this category of sound and it belongs with this alien. And then using brain imaging we were able to provide evidence to support the engagement of this [?] system.

But what it really was, was to create a portrait of her time.  So I had designed this idea in San Francisco there is this woman who came out of the hotel rooms. 

08:55:19	 I should tell you about that whole thing because in the late ‘60s I was asked to do an exhibition at the local museum because they didn’t start to show women.  They had never shown them. So I was supposed to do this show of drawings and instead I did these sculptures that talked, talked back to people.  Well the museum was horrified and closed the exhibition down like within three days. So I went there to see the show that was on. So I felt who needs the museum anyway. So Ellie and I we rented hotel rooms and created the artefacts of what it would be like for somebody to have lived in that hotel room and put the sound that was taken out of the museum into the closet.  So you would go and get a key at the desk, sign in get a key and trespass this alien space. So out of that I had to create the artefacts of somebody who lived in that neighbourhood.  And the show lasted almost a year and we did it just with little [?] signs that people saw so that they would know they had got 24 hours a day to see this exhibition in the [?] Hotel. And I started to think about it after that was closed by the police because somebody reported a murder in the room and they took [?] to the central station. I started to think about, well what if this character that you don’t see that you only identified by the things around here which in [?] they call negative space.  You see what you read, you see how she writes, you see her handwriting. What if she were to be liberated into the world and lived her own life in real time, in real space and gathered the artefacts of life into her collective fleshed out being that was going to be developed.  

	Of course when I explained it to people they thought you know, they thought it was crazy. So I ended up having to do it myself at least for the first 3-4 years and then she multiplied in there some brilliant multiples that went out. So it was this character that if you construct an archetype of the time, what would she look like?  What does the medium make women look like?  And she tried, she was assigned to reflect this so she had a background that was designed for her.  She had a history of really traumas. She came to San Francisco with a particular amount of money. She rented a room in the same hotel. And started to put ads in newspapers for roommates which is how she began what I call the Meet Mr America Series, and she recorded who would want to know her and she recorded everything. She had a tape recorder hidden in her purse. 

	So over time she fleshed out, she saw psychiatrists, she had a checking account. She had a drivers licence, she went to the dentist. She had psychiatric records.  She never met anybody more than three times because they tended to get attached to her and she didn’t want to be responsible for these people she was meeting.  But over time, over the course from 1972 to 1979 you saw her emerge as really a reflection of what was happening at the time.  She went to ESC meetings, she went to Weight Watchers and these things were all happening.  And the result of this was a series of about over 300 works including letters people wrote to her, letters she wrote in her own handwriting, her diary, her clothing. She always wore the same thing which was a particular shade of red which was like I think a forestation of the alchemical transformation.  

09:00:21	And all these artefacts exist and along with early drawings and paintings of what she would look like that was really comprised from media at the time, what women idealised women should look like. So all these artefacts exist and people can go through them and hear Roberta’s recording, look at her letters. Look at all the elements she left at the time. And in a sense when they looked through all this material they re-perform her. So it is nothing that never ends you know but is really again, a reflection and a witness to the times that she was consciously living through them. 

Question	And I long for her to re-surface now. There is no reason for her ever to die.  Although did she not have a funeral at one point?

Answer	No she was exorcised.  Because what was happening, around her fourth year we had multiple score out with her because Roberta was having very negative experiences.  You know she went to Weight Watchers and getting weighed, she went to ESC, she had her purse snatched. She went to the Santiago Zoo and a group that had answered her letter was a prostitution ring.  [laughing]  She had surveillance photographers documenting all this and also as a protection for me, I thought well nothing could really happen because these people are there. However they had so freaked out by this group of obvious people who wanted to lure her into their car to become part of their prostitution rings that they ran away. So the only way Roberta could become free was to go to the bathroom in Santiago Zoo and become Lynn.  And Lynn laughed and they were in there looking for the blond. So that was the tenuous escape she had from that particular kind of reality. But she was having negative experiences. So I thought okay well science, if you can prove something three times then there is probably some veracity to it. 

So I created three multiples that would go out dressed exactly like her.  And I chose dancers because they could mimic her stance and her gestures and so forth.  But they started to have negative experiences as well. So I thought okay, well after the decade she was fleshed out and had learned enough so that I could really have a mirror magnet refraction of what that time was like. And I could, I needed to find a way for her to survive beyond that.  So we did this exorcism and [?] versus crypt where she went in there and she did this little dance around the room, took her effigy of the construction chart which is what made her [?] and [?] stayed all night in the fog until it all disappeared. And that was how she converted from being the victim into someone of more empowered.  It was Lucrezia Borgia’s crypt that we did it in?

DAVID BIELLO, TED SCIENCE CURATOR
