NEIL GERSHENFELD
CENTRE FOR BITS AND ATOMS, MIT

Male	Do you want me looking at the camera? [Talking together]

Question
Male	Let’s talk about, I think [?] came to the workshop helped to make, were not necessarily looking to come from all [?] what they wanted to make, project.  What is so interesting in making why are people drawn to that?

Answer
Male	So there is a deep connection between making the class here and accidental global revolution I have been involved in. So I run the centre for Bits and atoms and IT that was started with the goal of having one of anything to make anything roughly, all the ways to go from digital to physical to look at that boundary. And the problem it would take a lifetime of classes just to learn how to use all the tools that we use. So I started with some colleagues the class modestly called how to make almost anything. And that was aimed at a few research students learning to use these advance research tools. And hundreds of students showed up to take the class for ten people. And it has happened every year since.
 
13:10:01	So it has become one of the most popular classes in MIT and we have expanded the multiple labs at MIT and even Harvard for the class.  And the real surprise was they weren’t there first and foremost to do research or to start businesses, they just wanted to create. And every year they would do the most amazing things.  So in that class I teach forming function, adding and subtractive 2D and 3D electronics code. All the ways just to create function. And the students do these amazing expressive things. The first year Kelly Dobson made a device to save up screams and play it back later when convenient. Like you can scream into it and it saves it up and releases it later by recording it and playing it back.  And somebody made a web browser for parrots and somebody made an alarm clock you wrestle with and somebody made a dress that defends your personal space. And these were so consistently so inventive I got, the students were answering a question I didn’t ask. And the research we do here we are looking at digital fabrication, how to go to digital, to physical. But not really why and they were showing, just as the killer app for digital computing was personal computing the killer app for digital fabrication was personal fabrication not production for mass markets. But production of market of one person.  Personal expression in technology.  And it touches something very, very deep. It is only in the modern era consumption has turned into clicking on web pages to purchase products made far away.  So it touches something very profound to be able to shape your physical environment.   

Question
Male	So you also write about revolution?

Answer	So computing went from single mainframes and in fact the first real time computer was made here at MIT called the Whirlwind, to mini computers that filled the room and the Whirlwind was commercialised as the PDP and that was used to create the internet. And from there on to [?] computers and personal computers and now smartphones and internet of things. And numerically that went from one to a thousand to a million to a billion to a trillion computing things. So that is what has happened in the last 50 years. And that is based on digital computing and communication. 

	In the same sense the research we are doing here is not driving a digital revolution in fabrication going from bits to atoms, literally digital to physical. And in the same way it is scaling in that same exponential. So it goes from IT the first computer controlled manufacturing was done in 1952 to fablabs that I will tell you about, to using fablabs to make fablabs to assemblers to self assemblers to the start track replicator.  And so we are looking at another 50 years of scaling from 1,000 to a million to a billion to a trillion. Now it is important about that historical background is email, video games, the internet, all the things you do with the mini computer.  You do today with the computer happened in the minicomputer era.  It didn’t happen after the smart phone, it happened when computing reached the scale of a work group. So that is where fablabs come in.  

13:15:29	Initially for the National Science Foundation, I set up a mini version of my lab here.  Instead of ten million dollars it is about a hundred thousand dollar investment which is a lot for one person but it is small for a community.  It is about two tons and fills a room.  It includes a 3D printer that gets a lot of attention, but includes all the other tools you need to make complete functional systems of boats, bicycles, furniture and consumer electronics and those accidentally went viral. So we set up one and that led to a second and that lead to another and so they have been doubling roughly every year and a half. There was over 1,500 of them.  Everywhere you can think of on earth.  And they are spreading very much like the spread of computing, but now crossing this digital to physical boundary. 

Question	So what is a fablab?

Answer	So fablabs are part of a much bigger story. There is maker movements and hacker spaces and all of that. But many of them are isolated that if you exercise in a gym you don’t benefit when somebody exercises in another gym.  But the fablab really works as a network.  So it is a curated set of capabilities to make almost anything and today that fills a big room, weighs two tons and costs 100,000 dollars. It includes again 3D printers, but precise milling, large format milling, laser cutters, moulding, casting, electronics, surface mount rework. All these tools you need to create technology. And this network of over 1,500 of the labs share this curated set of tools and then functions as a community and so we can talk about their classes over it. There is non profits, there are businesses, there are initiatives for urban self sufficiency. Programmes using this network to meet a wide range of local needs.  

Question	So who is it for?

Answer	Yes.  The answer is it is very similar to saying who is a computer for, how is it used?  It is used by. So if you take a typical fablab they teach classes, they have open free time for the community, they have little businesses, they help big businesses, the create community infrastructure, they have kids programmes, they have programmes for retired people.  Maybe one analogy is think about the role of a town library. Carnegie funded the creation of libraries at the turn of the previous century as driving a new kind of literacy.  This is now a new kind of literacy from going from digital to physical and has the same range of uses as a town library.

13:20:03
Question	So let’s talk about process of learning.  Is any teaching involved or is this all kind of self?

Answer	So there are a couple of kinds of teaching. The one thing it is not is simply self in that do it yourself is great in spirit but it also involves a lot of bad ideas and reinvention of things that are known to be poor ways to do things and better ways to do it.  What you need is mentoring but on the other hand traditional education is kind of just in case you build up educational inventory for the future rather than just in time learning where you learn project based as you go. So one of the core things the fablab network supports is mentoring. And the important part of that is a programme I run called the Fab academy. So if you look at the MIT behind me, the economic output of businesses from MIT campus fall between the economic output of India and Russia, it is like the world’s [?] economy which is a crazy number because it is not that many people and it is not that they are that brilliant but this is a productive environment for people like that. So in the same sense in the fablabs we have them in the arctic villages and African [?] town and India and they attract that same profile of [?] people who are often considered problems in local schools or businesses as they don’t follow the rules in them. And so we had a problem, they would come to the labs, start to learn skills and fall off an educational cliff. And so we started this programme called the Fab Academy building on the how to make anything class. I teach at MIT. So to understand that MIT is like a mainframe. You come here for processing and it is very productive for a very small number of people.  There is a lot of attention for online classes.  But those are in the history of computing like time sharing. There is still an educational mainframe and your terminal connected to it.  The way the Fab Academy works is students have peers with mentors in labs locally in these labs all around the world, but then we link them globally with real time video and content sharing. So we do a few hundred site video conferences and we have big platforms for content and sharing across these labs. So it is much like the internet. The internet unlike the Bitnet grew at the edges. You would add a computer to the edge of the internet for it to grow. And in the same sense the Fab Academy is a distributed network but it mentors going from beginning to hard, from easy to hard in an scalable distributed way.  

And one of the things that is very interesting in the Fab Academy is some of the sites are grass root community centres, some are companies, some are traditional formal institutions but they are all on an equal footing on this in participating in it. And it is turn has spun more classes. My colleague George Church started a class in this called How to grow almost anything to learn bio technology. And there is a textile technology class called fabri academy. There is a name that was kind of a joke, the academy of almost anything, or acadamany.  That refers to this collection of not do it yourself, not distance, not central but distributive learning. And for me that is one of the greatest implications of the fablab network because there are orders of magnitude and more just brain power and [?] of people we find than what flows into traditional education.  And this is a way to scale to meet that opportunity.   
12:26:02
Question	So what is the future of education?  

Answer	So I wrote an essay for the economic forum a few years ago and the question is MIT obsolete?  And my answer was 50% and what I mean by that is MIT has been very productive historically and there are some things that are really best done here that can’t be done anywhere else that require really specialised skills and really specialised capabilities. But traditional formal education is based on a lot of assumptions of scarcity that people, pools, books, space are all scarce so you have to limit access.  There is only a subset of learning that works by yourself looking at a computer screen. We have consistently found in the Fab Academy that isolated students really fail, they have to be in these learning work groups. And so what I meant by the 50% is that is you ask what can be done needs to be done in a place like MIT and what can be blown out to this distributed educational model. For comparison I helped plan a building at MIT that cost ten years to make and ended up being almost a thousand dollars a square foot to build. And a building like that, a hundred million dollar investment makes a few hundred people happy.  At that same time I helped create over a thousand fablabs each making a hundred people happy and so then the question is what can be done in that distributed way and what needs a more central education and that boundary is an interesting one.  There is a boundary but the boundary keeps receding as kind of what I am doing globally in the fablab network competes with what I am doing at MIT. And MIT is very happy to have me compete me with myself like that because the real goal is furthering the aims of it.  

	But it is important to understand we didn’t set out to create this global educational network, we started by pairing with all kinds of existing educational programmes but we kept hitting limits because the core competence of MIT is really cultural.  It is a place where people are productive in the same sense the people we were attracting to the fablabs these really bright and creative people kept running into businesses and schools that would strangle them, that would limit how they do it. 

13:30:28	So this environment where you can do [?] your aim reduction to practice learning but with mentoring is really bringing the social engineering, the skills to create productive environments, for this outlier people in a much more scalable way. 

Question	But it is not the case of fablabs, micro MIT you are talking about different model. Why is it kind of more traditional model? The other one is more distributed knowledge system?

Answer	But the interesting this is exactly that boundary because Fablabs began as outreach and whatever is doing it, MIT began as research but really a great example is every year I teach the How to make anything class at MIT and we have hundreds of students trying to get into it. And then I teach the global version in the Fab Academy and bit by bit the global version is beginning to get ahead of the MIT version in that tapping more brain power of more of the planet.  And so if you look at not do it yourself and not working in an isolated lab, but using this whole lab network as a distributed campus it is beginning to climb up from outreach to substantial technical education to beginning to bump up against doing research. And so if you look at history from mainframes to Bitnet to internet, the smart thermostat today has the computational capability of the early min computer and so in the same way these educational networks are really bumping up against that boundary between traditional formal education. 

One of the most interesting signs of that is, a few years back I did a key note for educos a group that runs EDU and it was a lovely session, but then we talked about accreditation about how to accredit the fab academy. But they loved what we were doing but they said they couldn’t do it because you accredit a place you can’t accredit a network.  They had no way to do that. But they said something very helpful which is they encouraged us to make up our own accreditation. So we invented a degree that we give in the fablab network that is just made up. But it is very, very carefully evaluated. The students have mentors supervising them. There is a regional [?] checking those there are global oversight of them.  It is very carefully curated with skills based portfolio. And we get asked a lot of questions, but not who authorised to give that because nobody really knows how to do it, but we build documentation for it. What is happening now is this made up degree we teach all over the world in these distributed sites, a number of traditional universities like the [?] in Paris or [?] in Finland are actually giving their credentials to the students coming through this process so you can be a [?] student in the [?] while participating this global fab academy.  

Again really challenging and blurring the boundary between traditional formal education and this distributed one. There is a lot of false dichotomy so one is traditional versus do it yourself, one is classroom versus online.  What I am describing is none of those. It is mentoring educational networks. 
13:35:29
Question	Can we go back to making, the role of making in education and also project based learning as that is a big thing?

Answer	A couple of parts to that. One is if you ask a random person do you want to learn a dance computational design or path planning for machine tools they might look at you blankly.  But if you ask a random person would you like to design and create your own furniture?  It just touches something really, really deep. The fablabs keep spreading because nobody is pushing them but somebody sees them and wants to go to somewhere else. But I described in the lab you can do classes, you can do businesses, you can do community outreach, you can create infrastructure, you can do play you can do art. What we find is if you segregate those the labs fail.  There are very strong synergies. So the education in the lab can’t be isolated from the rest of the lab. The education is part of the life of the lab, but you need all those other activities going on. If you just call it an educational lab it flounders. A colleague in Detroit is doing really, Blair Evans is doing really interesting work where you hear about the bankrupt economy and he is setting up labs where in the lab a third of the time is traditional economic work, a third is post salary economy it is post [?] exchange, it is things for the community but not denominated in dollars. And a third is enrichment, you growing benefiting the community but not for money.  In the same way there is a Fab city initiative that started in Barcelona where they have a great design sense, but 50% youth unemployment. So there is a group of cities signing up to provide these tools as urban infrastructure to turn consumption into creation. Or we are [?] where the economy is based on not gross domestic product, but gross national happiness.  It doesn’t mean they are happy, but it means you measure well being.

	In each of those cases it is really getting at these very fundamental questions about what is work, what is money?  Instead of jobs for work, for money for income, for consumption, being able to sustainably produce locally.  Connecting all of them is continuous life time learning.  But the learning is not an isolated segregated task, it is part of the integrated life of these labs.

Question	Part of meaningful life, adds meaning?

Answer	Well I mean it goes all the way back to birds creating nests, that is a species, something very deep in us that wants to shape our physical environment. And it is only in the relatively recent era that scrolls out so we become consumers. In a way this is back to the past. But the crucial distinction is you need to be connected globally but fabricate locally.  This rests on not global supply chains to ship finished products, but global supply chains of knowledge to share that enables the ability to sustainably produce locally.

Answer

Answer
Female	So at that time the way most people would interact with these dialogue systems was via the telephone.  And so you had access to the speech.  It wasn’t necessarily the highest quality, but you could analyse just the speech signal for example prosodic characteristics and you could measure things like the pitch range so how much their voice properties varied. You could measure how loudly they were talking. You could look at puzzle patterns, how much silence and things like that. And by measuring those and then using some statistical algorithm such as machine learning and having some training data where you have already labelled where people think these states were occurring. You could learn predictive models.  

If you also had a speech recognition system that worked well in that domain then you could get the transcripts. That idea is once you develop the models then you can deploy them so as people are speaking you can apply the models and when these states happen, you can change the behaviour of the system to respond to them.   

05:05:29
Question	So in terms of the kind of linguistic, excuse my bad vocabulary, but recognising patterns, identifying, you mentioned various vocabulary. This is all things that humans need to do that students, researchers, professors are doing in order to set up structures which can then be programmed into the computer, that aggregating lots and lots of those models and lots and lots of things that set examples of where speech has had particular results, is what the machine learning can do. What the kind of computing power can do, right?

Answer	Kind of yeah. I mean there are different types of machine learning. So one type is called supervised learning. So you basically need training data with the same input and the target that you are looking for.  And then in the old days, just a few years ago, before [?] net took over. The key aspect of getting the systems to work was what is called feature engineering, for example to take the speech or the text, the transcript and to re-represent that in terms of features that are the output of different kind of programmes, either speech programmes or natural language programmes. And then those would become the input to the machine learning models. And so for them to work you need a lot of data to do that.  

Now there are these new techniques, deep learning and oral nets which learn the re-presentations so you can kind of skip some of that middle phase of doing that feature engineering. Because those methods do require a lot of data and particularly in my field of educational applications, it is very hard to get large amounts of data, do lots of privacy issues and things with student data.  There are other machine learning techniques which really help supervise where you need a little data and that kind of boot straps the process or totally unsupervised they typically have lower performance, but kind of trading them off with the data. 

There is also work called reinforcement learning approaches which is more trial and error. So instead of like having a data set, you basically just try things and then you will get rewards or punished.  And so by just trying things out you can learn over time and we have also used that in some of our dialogue work.  So because you can kind of build a good baseline system, but there are lots of choices maybe that you don’t know the right one. And so that way you can build a system and have it explore the space of possible choices and then maybe it learns. So for example in a tutoring system you could learn. If you had some measure of learning for example, which behaviour from the dial out system, lets students to learn more after they have interacted with the system.

Question
Female	So let’s start out with what needed to be done, the kind of objective.  Tell us about the objective of that in terms of speech recognition for a telephone usage please?

Answer
Female	Okay so back in the day they were kind of hand designed, basically call phones used like some sort of formal representation that you said this and these expected responses happened and you were just kind of traverse that and maybe old enough to remember the old systems where you would like press one and then you would get that and then maybe keep asking for an idea behind like systems, you could go down a very slow dialogue progression that was equivalent to that, but instead of saying press one, you would actually give your answer in language but that you could also skip all those steps and get sort of down to the bottom because language is so much more expressive that you could say what you wanted in one sentence rather than in parts.

	The problem with that was that it was very fixed. And so what we were interested in was how could we track dynamically as the conversation was progressing to see how it was going. And if it wasn’t going as we had hoped we would do things differently. So one way you could do things differently is just give up on the conversation totally and pass them to a human operator. Another way you could do things differently was you could change the behaviour of the dialogue system. So some of the dimensions we looked at were something called initiative, which is how kind of focused the questions are. So you could say ‘How may I help you?’  and that gives a very open ended answer. Or like in a travel kind of scenario you could say, if you were trying to book a flight let’s say. ‘What city do you want to go to?’ ‘What city are you leaving from?’ ‘How much time?’ and you ask things one by one which tends to work. Or at that time tended to work better from a speech recognition perspective because the space of the answers was so restricted, but it took much longer. So you kind of wanted to find the sweet spot of giving the user the most flexibility, but having the technology work. 
05:16:07
	And so we tried to use on speech and language processing to track various properties of other answers to estimate things as the dialogue was progressing. And then when things didn’t seem to be going as expected we would progressively go to these more conservative strategies. So we would ask more focused questions. We would actually confirm everything. So after they said something you could say, did you say this, say yes or no. Or there are different types of ways of confirming. And we tried to learn how to optimise the design of the system from the data we were collecting over time.

Female	Can we move on to talk about education because as you have told us about your career it seems you move towards education now. Answer	Right. So when I moved here the first work I did was in an area called tutorial dialogue systems where the idea was for example we developed a system for teaching qualitative physics where you were taught, the student would solve the physics problem, not involving equations, but more conceptually. And then we would work them through the solution by having a conversation. So the idea was human tutors used that form of teaching. And so we were trying to see that spoken dialogue technology could be applied in that context. And so from the technical perspective it was very similar, but instead of like looking for when customers were getting frustrated, we were interested in looking where the students were getting bored and might not be paying attention or when they were uncertain. 

So not just processing the dialogue with respect to the physics content which would usually help people, you would ask a question you know. What is gravity and they would give an answer. And it was either right or wrong. What we were interested in was more nuanced analysis. So they might give the right answer, but you could tell from their voice that they are very hesitant or they are saying it like a question.  And so we wouldn’t want the tutor to respond exactly the same even though the words were exactly the same. So that was kind of the analogy to the ATNT work. The context was very different and also from a person interested in building dialogue systems, it was a nice twist on the technology because there were so many properties that were different that applying the prior work just directly didn’t really work. So ATNT were interested in very short conversations because they wanted throughput.

And learning you actually would like the students to talk more. And so we wanted to keep them.  And now with interest in chatbots too, people actually want longer conversations that people hadn’t really looked at such long conversations at that time.

So after we do the scoring we then have, based on the model of why they got that score which is tied to the grading, we knew what they did well and what they did less well. And so we have deployed that over the last year and a half and we are analysing how well our system can help the students improve how they are using their feedback, what the teachers can do with that should they interact with the feedback and go around and kind of amplify it or how they can best use that. CYNTHIA GOLDEN AND SERAPHIM THORNTON

07:05:15	[New interview]

Female	I am a little trained in being in front of a camera so my instinct is to go – and now we are going to talk about DNA [laughing]
	
	So virtual reality is a really interesting emerging technology that basically allows the user to be immersed in the situation.

	[Interrupt]

	So virtual reality is a really interesting emerging technology where the user can really be immersed in the situation you would like them to experience. There are a couple of different kinds of virtual reality today.  One on one end of the spectrum is 360 degree video where you put your camera when you are making the video that you want your users to experience. You put your camera in the middle of a room and, or in the middle of a scene or wherever you feel like your shot is really best organised. And then the camera takes video all around the camera. So the camera can see straight up, it can see straight down, it can see behind itself. And so when the user is experiencing that video, the user can’t decide where to move, the user is only where the camera is from the shot.  But they can decide where to look.

	And so it is a really interesting perspective because it is not like traditional film making where you can decide where your user or your viewer is going to look, how they experience the story. Rather you are putting them in a situation and allowing them to decide how the story is going to unfold for them. And every time that user goes through the experience, they can decide to look at a different thing. For example we have someone interested in using 360 degree video in a surgical operating room. And they want to put a camera in there so that they can have one video that is recording the whole surgery, but different people who are being trained for different roles can watch the different people with the different roles as they move around the room.  

	Or maybe they are being trained in all of the roles or how to have the team work together as a functional unit. And they can watch all the different people in different run through of this video.  

	On the other end of virtual reality you have things like the oculist rift which is a piece of technology that allows you to experience a, it is usually somewhere in the realm of a video but also a simulation where not only can you look all around you as the user, but you can also decide to move around in this space. So if I am standing here and there is a column over here I can go and I can look behind that column, that is something I can’t do in 360 degree video.  

	And also in many virtual reality experiences, depending on how they have been programmed you can interact with your environment. So for example you can fly around a city like an eagle or you can solve a puzzle in order to get out of a car before it explodes.  Or you can raise a car and you can actually effect your environment based on what you are doing inside it.

	And so it has a lot of promise in terms of educational benefits.  The research is really just getting started, but there is a lot of it out there already. One piece that I found particularly interesting is that if you put a user inside a virtual environment and ask them to memorise things about that environment, possibly things that you have planted in that environment like words or pictures or phrases. They are better at memorising and retaining those things that you have asked them to memorise in that environment, in virtual reality than they are if you just show them this scene on a computer screen. So they don’t feel like they are in it.  And a lot better than if you just like put a list on a piece of paper.

07:10:48
	So it is something about the body and the brain really being tricked into feeling like they are there because our brains evolved to learn about our environment. We didn’t evolve to learn about this on pieces of paper. And so the closer you can get what you want someone to retain, to being part of what their brain perceives as their environment, the better for retention. 

	[laughing]

Male	About five minutes!

Answer
Sarah	One of my parts of cognitive science is that we learn better when visuals are included and not just words. And this is basically universal in terms of all different subjects and all different sorts of people in different walks of life. If you combine visuals in with your words you understand better, you learn better, you retain better. And this is something that takes a lot of effort to do because it not only is a picture of worth 1,000 words. But a picture takes a 1,000 times the length of writing the words that would otherwise describe that thing.  And it becomes even more complicated if you make that picture move. So for example if you have an animation or you create a video like this one, and so a lot of people don’t take advantage of this fact because it takes so much effort to make good visuals. Because if you make bad visuals they are not going to contribute to learning, you have to actually do it right.  

There is a lot known about how to make visuals that aren’t good for learning, for example, [?] principles are like the basics that I try to start everyone out with. Professor Mayer works for the University of California and he has done a lot of research over the years, over the course of his career into what makes good visuals, what makes good video, what makes good multi-media for learning. And also incorporated other people research and kind of distilled it down to approximately 12 principles. And some of the things are very intuitive like you know we learn. Well the first principle is we learn better when you combine images with your words and that is multi-media.  And some of them are things like we learn better from a human voice than we do from a robotic voice. We learn better from someone who is talking very personally as opposed to someone who is talking very formally. You know use contractions, be enthusiastic, be personal. Don’t just try to make everything sound very formal and professional and stilted as many people have the thought that they should do.

And so if you go into all of this evidence based knowledge that we have and put that together and actually make multi-media that is something that really has been shown to make learning a lot more effective.  

	It is a part of our job here is to help faculty learn about that so that they can incorporate those kind of approaches into their teaching.  
 
Cynthia[?]	When you have an emotional response you are more likely to remember it and retain that information. And negative responses are more retainable than positive reactions. And so how that could be incorporated into learning, especially with VR is VR creates a much more responses and as such you are more likely to retain everything that you learn in VR because of the emotional response that you have and also the use of a bunch of different senses which can help retain information.

NICK NYSTROM, PITT SUPER COMPUTER
University of Pittsburgh, May 2019
TC: 7:30:34 or a little after

Male	A super computer is a just in for processing numbers and data. By most definitions it is at least two orders of magnitude of one hundred times more capable than the systems people otherwise have access to.  So if most individuals have a very nice PC or workstation, a super computer would be something at least a hundred times stronger than that. And what that lets you do is it lets you do something that is not just one hundred times faster or one hundred times what you would get if you put one hundred different computers together. But it lets you solve problems that otherwise are inaccessible and really can being the barrier to things that otherwise we have to wait say five or ten years to do. Instead you can do them right now. And that’s its real value. It lets you do things now instead of waiting for technology to advance. 

Question
Female	And when we talk about machine learning, can you only do machine learning. Can you talk about that?

Answer
Male	So the concept of machine learning and supercomputing is really interesting. Here at the Pittsburgh [??] centre we pioneered the convergence of bringing together machine learning and supercomputing where high performance computing it is currently called, with a system called Bridges.   

Most machine learning right now is done on desktops and laptops using something called server processing units. Those together with big data are what allowed machine learning to become so important as it is today. What we have here in Pittsburgh is a system that really let’s people scale that up. And so if people want to do machine learning, not just on a small collection of images from the internet or a section of voice recordings, but they want to do it on a [?] scale genome atlas.  That is what you do on a super computer when you bring machine learning to it. And to make that possible we have not only hundreds of GPUs or graphics process units together, we also have other general purpose processers for doing all the data pre-processing, data explaining in preparation. And the facilities for getting at many petabytes of data very efficiently.  And those are things that you can’t do on a desktop because on a desktop you have terabytes not petabytes which is thousands of times larger.   

Question
Female	Right. Can you talk us through a likely sample exercise.  What do I mean?  Like exercise. If someone CMU or Pitt has a particular thing they want to do and huge data set they want to extract something from. 

Sample projects
How do they do it and at what point do they take it or send it over to the computers themselves?  What is the process there?

Answer
Male	The workflow for dealing with very large data. We serve researchers across the country and across the world. We have about 1,900 projects running right now. And about 15,000 users.  The typical workflow though is that people have big data and that can come from different sources. It can come from say genome sequencers, it can come from the internet, from other scientific experiments or from socioeconomic data. 
07:35:13
Typically they realise they have a bottleneck and they bring it here. An example of this was a researcher at the University of Pittsburgh working in Slavic languages and literatures.  This was not a field that typically used supercomputing and when we built our new system bridges we talked to him and he is tracing the flow of information through Europe in the dark ages.  And you can do that by looking at these documents called Slavic miscellany which are prayer books, stories, histories and they were hand written by Monks. And so as a story moves and is retold, it accumulates changes. And you can use that almost like looking at the way gene evolves to look at its linage. When the researcher first talked to me he said he was doing about fifty, working with fifty of these works. And I said it seems small, why only fifty?  And he said that is what fits on my laptop. And we talked for a few minutes and then I asked him, how many of them are there really? And he said there were about 500,000.  He will never get to those in his lifetime. 

So we talked and I explained to him what a supercomputer can do and how it works. And about three minutes later you could just see the light bulb turn on and he realised he could do it all right now. And so the way people work with this is they have data, they move it to us by a variety of ways. It can be over the internet. Most people just do that and it is fine. We have received large brain electro microscopy images from Harvard, get about three terabytes a day. We have had other people bring briefcases full of hard drives because from their lab they don’t have a good network. And that works for them. We have a project called the Brain Image Library which is [?] an national institutes of health. For that we deal with many labs that don’t have good networks. So we have developed a portable storage solution called the Brain Ball because it is roughly [?].  and we just FedEx it to them. They fill it with data and we bring it back.

07:40:08	And then comes the actual machine learning.  A lot of that now is usually what is called deep learning.  Deep learning is a technique that really took off around 2012 as a result of an image processing competition which it was first really applied using graphics processing which is for GPUs. And at that time it catapulted the accuracy from what had been a very level rate of 70 some percent and increasing only by half a percent per year. Because some increased accuracy to more like 90%. And so that represents 40 years of human progress based on previous curve. And since then has been greatly refined. And so we are finding that has tremendous use across many fields. We are working in projects with immunology, with the human body at cellular resolution.  People are doing a lot with speech translation. A tremendous amount of medical imaging with digital pathology for example. Being able to characterise whether microscopic images are cancerous or not or represent other disease.

	And so one place where we see great opportunity is imagine you want to trace the way the neurons are connected in the brain. You would like to give a data set that is all of the slices in three dimensions at very high resolution where you can see individual neurons and what you would like to come out of that is identification of what tissues are in those images and then connect them in three dimensions to give you the map of how the neurons are connected. Because in neural networks today those are relatively monolithic, relatively uniform. The brain is more hierarchical and there may be emerging behaviour that comes from that hierarchy to understanding how the cortex works, would be a great application of these methods. And the reason they are needed is the standard technique today is graduate students sitting in front of a monitor clicking on each neuron one by one and manually identifying them and then it is manually build in new 3D structure. And that just doesn’t scale.  The Brain Image Library we are assembling here will have 10 pedabytes of data to start. So 10 pedabytes think 2,000 laptops.  And that is just the beginning. Part of it is from the richness of the connections right now between two are official neurons.  There is a [?] weight and the weight represents the strength of their connection.  Between neurons and our brains, there are many different protein pathways and there are more like 20 or more reactions that are occurring between them to mediate those connections. 	The other thing is our brains do what is called spiking. Neurons do not [?] all once. These algorithms, everything runs at the same time. Our brains neurons are activated based on the voltage spike and they activate the next neurons in the chain.  That has been developed in what is called neuro morphic processing.  That is in its infancy. It will require new algorithms. There is new hardware being developed by a number of companies and research groups and we will see where that goes. And then there is also a lot of promise for doing certain kinds of machine learning with quantum computing.  That is I think further out because it is depending on several different technologies all moving ahead. But there is a lot of potential that is yet unexplored. And what we are seeing now is very promising, but it is also the tip of the iceberg.

	In machine learning there are really three different kinds of learning and they are supervised learning where there is a what we call labelled dataset. And the system trains on that and you can assess whether it is right or wrong based on the labels.  So the set pictures in the picture of the label, the cat, dog, Frisbee and so on. And it is supervised, if it is necessarily wrong. Unsupervised is when it just learns from the data and it is learning completely on its own without getting feedback. Some supervise is in the middle where a human is particularly a human in the loop is giving occasional feedback. And all these have value in different settings. One of the biggest challenges in machine learning is often not having enough data to work with. Now this can happen for example, we are doing a project now with development of the immune system. And these have gotten so good that the images they produce would for those people while they are tiny artefacts things aren’t quite right, but that would be I think fixed within the next few months by the time this airs. And so these are ways of getting around not enough data. All of this leads to competence. And competence is a word for assessing whether or not the AI with a machine learning system has reached what seems to be a human level of ability or competence. So can it caption videos, can it recognise it in this video is a family overlooking the waterfall, and give their caption for you. And that is where we are now. I think we are going to see us bringing it to additional fields and as we get this additional competence this is where it becomes a really interesting application for education because this will let us have systems that will really help teachers.
